        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer429175464" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3786822431814212981"><i>There's something dangerous about the boredom of teenage girls.</i><br/><br/>The thought of getting older is something that scares the bejesus out of me every day I look in the mirror and see a new gray hair or small wrinkle, but there is noooooo amount of money in the world that would make me want to be a teenage girl in today's society. Girls have found all sorts of new ways to be vicious to one another, high schools have become war zones, and the pressure to be thin, beautiful, and perfect, has surpassed a</span>
  <span id="freeText3786822431814212981" style="display:none"><i>There's something dangerous about the boredom of teenage girls.</i><br><br>The thought of getting older is something that scares the bejesus out of me every day I look in the mirror and see a new gray hair or small wrinkle, but there is noooooo amount of money in the world that would make me want to be a teenage girl in today's society. Girls have found all sorts of new ways to be vicious to one another, high schools have become war zones, and the pressure to be thin, beautiful, and perfect, has surpassed anything I could have imagined as a teenager. <br><br>Megan Abbott's novel isn't just about average "mean girls". DARE ME is set in the back-stabbing and competitive world of American high-school cheerleading...and believe me there are nooooo spirit fingers here...<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1389811609i/8056356.jpg" alt=" photo 09555d08-7bd3-488a-8b7c-95676ee9b17a_zpsc7e870f6.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>DARE ME is narrated by 16-year-old Adelaine (Addy)- who at the start of the story is best "frenemies" and lieutenant to Beth- the manipulative and emotionally abusive captain of their school's cheerleading squad- until the arrival of an attractive and exciting new coach- Colette French. As Beth starts losing control of Addy and the rest of her team- coach becomes "the enemy" who must be destroyed.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1389811609i/8056357.jpg" alt=" photo ae29d09f-e525-443c-b820-0b88a0079425_zps5d045a16.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This is NOT the Rah Rah cheerleading I remember from my high school, and DARE ME is NOT the YA reading I was doing in my teenage years. I am not a parent- if I was I would cringe a little if I saw this book on my teenager's night stand- but as an adult I loved it. It has been billed as Fight Club for girls...but it reminded me more of Heathers meets Bring It On. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1389811709i/8056367.jpg" alt=" photo cca36a78-708f-46d7-b794-52154fe6d69f_zps5c7ac4f0.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Mystery, secrets, extreme paranoia, sex, loss of innocence, diet pill popping, smoothies and murder. Once you start reading, it is hard to put down.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer18287686078642902296">Wow. This was just really, really bad. I knew 10 pages in, that this book, definately wasn't for me, but I hate not finishing a book, no matter how godawful it is {example: 50 Shades of <s>shite</s> Grey}.<br/><br/>Yes the author throws in some typical high school speech {see beyotch etc.} to show the readers that she's down with the lingo, but the majority of the dialogue between the characters was ridiculous.   <span id="freeText18287686078642902296" style="display:none">Wow. This was just really, really bad. <div id="review_1552542167" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1552542167" />
    <a title="Debbie &quot;DJ&quot;" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12620745-debbie-dj"><img alt="Debbie &quot;DJ&quot;" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407426768p2/12620745.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1552542167?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Debbie &quot;DJ&quot;" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Debbie &quot;DJ&quot;" href="/user/show/12620745-debbie-dj">Debbie &quot;DJ&quot;</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12620745-debbie-dj?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12620745-debbie-dj?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1552542167" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer359524309441463281">I'm having a heck of a time trying to review this one! Megan Abbott's writing is brilliant, and she can get into the psyche of teenage girls like nothing I've ever read. While her writing is as amazing as ever here, the plot didn't thrill me. This story goes into the world of high school cheerleading, and holy shit was I ever surprised! This is not the cheerleading I know, where girls ruffle pom-poms and smile. No, this involves dare devil stunts where injuries can be fatal. <br/><br/>Abbott writes: <br/>"Now </span>
  <span id="freeText359524309441463281" style="display:none">I'm having a heck of a time trying to review this one! Megan Abbott's writing is brilliant, and she can get into the psyche of teenage girls like nothing I've ever read. While her writing is as amazing as ever here, the plot didn't thrill me. This story goes into the world of high school cheerleading, and holy shit was I ever surprised! This is not the cheerleading I know, where girls ruffle pom-poms and smile. No, this involves dare devil stunts where injuries can be fatal. <br /><br />Abbott writes: <br />"Now we are learning the pyramid isn't about girls climbing on top of one another and staying still. It's about breathing something to life. Together. Each of us a singular organ feeding the other organs, creating something larger. We are learning that our bodies are our own and they are the squads and that is all. We are learning that we are the only people in the world when we are on the floor. We will wear our smiles tight and meaningless, but inside, all we care for is stunt. Stunt is all." <br /><br />She goes on to describe the relationships between the girls, and in particular the squad leader and her best friend, also on the squad. These relationships are all consuming, fierce, unnerving. The squad leader is the top of the food chain. She calls all the shots, who's in, who's out. She says jump and they ask off which bridge. I found this amazing, and how Abbott describes these relationships blew my socks off!<br /><br />Okay, now plot. Well, here we have a murder committed that may involve the coach. The squad leader and her best friend are at odds, jealousy is rampant between them as the best friend and coach get to close. While the writing nails the deep emotions between all three, I found the story a little far fetched. It did show me how boundaries get crossed between coaches and their "girls," but I wasn't thrilled with how a murder became the driving force. I've always been one for great writing though, so while this may not be my favorite Abbott book, I'll be sure to read more of her!</span>
  <a data-text-id="359524309441463281" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_389428503" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/389428503" />
    <a title="Trudi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1416912-trudi"><img alt="Trudi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1446613110p2/1416912.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/389428503?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trudi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trudi" href="/user/show/1416912-trudi">Trudi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=crime-mystery">crime-mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=2013">2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=megan-abbott">megan-abbott</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=noir-is-the-new-black">noir-is-the-new-black</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=heart-of-darkness">heart-of-darkness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=girls-behaving-badly">girls-behaving-badly</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=twss">twss</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer389428503" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12734943230490788282"><br/>I've read gobs of creepy books and watched heaps of horror movies, but nothing can run a spike of scare through me quite like a gaggle of teen girls. You knew these things already, didn't you? Or at least suspected -- the vicious, petty jealousies, the unchecked hormones, the cutting intelligence harnessed to manipulate and intimidate, the capricious cruelty, the fathomless insecurities, the abiding self-loathing ... need I go on? <br/><br/>Teen girls are a tribe unto themselves, with their own language, </span>
  <span id="freeText12734943230490788282" style="display:none"><br />I've read gobs of creepy books and watched heaps of horror movies, but nothing can run a spike of scare through me quite like a gaggle of teen girls. You knew these things already, didn't you? Or at least suspected -- the vicious, petty jealousies, the unchecked hormones, the cutting intelligence harnessed to manipulate and intimidate, the capricious cruelty, the fathomless insecurities, the abiding self-loathing ... need I go on? <br /><br />Teen girls are a tribe unto themselves, with their own language, dress code, rules of behavior, and very specific rites of passage. Every day is Lord of the Flies day for teen girls. They don't need no stinking island to channel their inner savage, alright? Stephen King knew this when he has Carrie White cornered and bleeding from her first menses in the girl's high school locker room while her classmates pelt her with sanitary napkins and tampons chanting: "Plug it up! Plug it up! Plug it up!" <br /><br />In <i>Dare Me</i>, Megan Abbott takes you <i>deep</i> into teen girl territory, so deep you will flinch, and grimace and squirm at all the things she's going to show you. It's a sordid voyeurism that will have you screaming for more. This isn't a darkly humorous satire a la <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0377092/?ref_=fn_al_tt_1" rel="nofollow noopener">Mean Girls</a> or <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0097493/" rel="nofollow noopener">Heathers</a>. Not at all. This is a sober, penetrating look at the inner lives of a group of cheerleaders -- their insular, isolated existence as members of a tribe within a tribe. Their rituals include starvation diets and brute, physical demands requiring near constant pain and risk of serious injury. <br /><br />Into a volatile balance of power comes new Head Coach Collette French. Loyalties shift, boundaries are tested, trusts will be broken and amidst all the angst and perpetual drama, a body will be discovered. For Ms. Abbott isn't just writing cheerleaders, she's writing <i>noir</i> cheerleaders, with a rich cast of characters each vying for the role of femme fatale. <br /><br />This is a story icky in parts dealing as it does in burgeoning, pubescent sexuality, obsessions and desires. For all of that it is bloody <i>fascinating</i> featuring an engaging plot that Abbott has exquisitely paced. I read this in one sitting. I was violently pulled into this world and held captive the entire time. This isn't a happy story. You've been warned. <br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="12734943230490788282" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3645997641" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3645997641" />
    <a title="Cristina Monica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14596170-cristina-monica"><img alt="Cristina Monica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589617514p2/14596170.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3645997641?book_show_action=true">Nov 15, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cristina Monica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cristina Monica" href="/user/show/14596170-cristina-monica">Cristina Monica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14596170-cristina-monica?shelf=friendship-drama">friendship-drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14596170-cristina-monica?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14596170-cristina-monica?shelf=sports">sports</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14596170-cristina-monica?shelf=high-school">high-school</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3645997641" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14422182070740985404">I’m obsessed. It’s rare that I read one book and already I know I want to read everything the author has ever written and follow her on every account, but that’s how I feel right now about Megan Abbott after reading the crazy rollercoaster of psychological manipulation that is DARE ME. <br/><br/>It’s not even that it’s a perfect novel; hence the lack of a five-star rating, but it’s addictive in a way that some people are. Some people you meet and already from the very beginning you notice their flaws, but</span>
  <span id="freeText14422182070740985404" style="display:none">I’m obsessed. It’s rare that I read one book and already I know I want to read everything the author has ever written and follow her on every account, but that’s how I feel right now about Megan Abbott after reading the crazy rollercoaster of psychological manipulation that is DARE ME. <br /><br />It’s not even that it’s a perfect novel; hence the lack of a five-star rating, but it’s addictive in a way that some people are. Some people you meet and already from the very beginning you notice their flaws, but there’s just so much life and excitement and unexpectedness in them that you can’t help but want to see them again and will deal with their flaws if it means you get to have them in your life. <br /><br />So while there are definitely things that could have been improved, I’m not sure WHAT should have been improved because every single aspect of this story adds to its tense atmosphere and twisted scenes. I guess the mystery could have started earlier, if this is why you’re considering reading this book, but I for one adored being introduced to the world of cheerleading in high school and the intricate relationships between the cheerleaders.<br /><br />I never really fully got the deal between Beth, Addy and their parents, and the coach will forever remain a mystery, but Megan Abbott tells us just enough to be intrigued and want more. She’s a tease in many ways and I kind of loved it. There is one aspect of the mystery that I figured out before Addy, so that was a slight bummer, but I read it more because I was dying to see how Beth and Addy’s friendship will fare then because I wanted the murderer exposed. <br /><br />In sum, don’t let the low average rating discourage you from reading this! Unless you really, really, really care what other people think and can’t make up your own mind. Okay, that was a bit rude, but that’s a taste of Beth for you. You’ll love her, she’s a doll.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14422182070740985404" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_689587697" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/689587697" />
    <a title="Rose" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3399699-rose"><img alt="Rose" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386022874p2/3399699.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/689587697?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rose" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rose" href="/user/show/3399699-rose">Rose</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=better-than-i-expected">better-than-i-expected</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=suspense">suspense</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=glbt">glbt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=realistic-fiction">realistic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer689587697" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8906289895736605062">I wrote this review while playing Nancy Sinatra's "Bang Bang (My Baby Shot Me Down)." There are reasons why that's appropriate, but that might delve into spoilerific territory, so I digress.<br/><br/>Forewarning: this will be a long review, possibly one breaking the character limit. That might be surprising considering I'm giving this (close to) 5-stars. In the aftermath of reading this, I will definitely be reading more of Megan Abbott's work. No question.<br/><br/><b>Short version of this review: I freaking loved th</b></span>
  <span id="freeText8906289895736605062" style="display:none">I wrote this review while playing Nancy Sinatra's "Bang Bang (My Baby Shot Me Down)." There are reasons why that's appropriate, but that might delve into spoilerific territory, so I digress.<br /><br />Forewarning: this will be a long review, possibly one breaking the character limit. That might be surprising considering I'm giving this (close to) 5-stars. In the aftermath of reading this, I will definitely be reading more of Megan Abbott's work. No question.<br /><br /><b>Short version of this review: I freaking loved this book. Problematic as all heck - I realize - but that was a ride I would want to ride again, over and over. Willingly. </b><br /><br />The long version of this review gets a bit more complicated because I'll acknowledge this book has significant caveats and (notably) won't strike everyone the same way. It is entirely a love it or hate it read, and it's problematic to say the least (understatement). I understand why, and I'd understand anyone's hesitation to pick it up. I should also mention that while this features a teen protagonist, this is very much an adult book. (I definitely think there are teens who would appreciate this read for what it offers. Considering the content, though, it's a hard bargain. Discretion advised.) <br /><br />I'd honestly say this is one to give a try at the very least because...dude. Let's have a conversation.<br /><br />"Dare Me" was yet another library recommended read that I randomly picked up in the context of recs made in the scheme of "Gone Girl." As you guys know, I take comparison reads very lightly. More often than not, they're not always accurate and can set up unrealistic expectations. In the aftermath of reading this, I somewhat see why the rec came about. <b>For one, it's a very dark read - the tension in "Dare Me" is so thick wading through it, you find yourself lost in its pull of the trainwreck variety. I could not stop reading this book once I started. Even putting it down, I wanted to pick it back up again because I had no idea where the heck it was going. It has more than its fair share of twists and turns. </b><br /><br /><b>Another similarity: the characters are supremely unlikable.</b> I didn't like a single character in this book, not even the protagonist Addy (who arguably has some sympathetic qualities, she's quite the anti-hero). So you might wonder why I give close to five stars for a book that's essentially about a bunch of pretentious, self-absorbed teenage cheerleaders abusing Adderall and doing everything they can to maintain slim figures and championing unhealthy eating habits and eating disorder behavior? <br /><br /><b>Maybe it's because I'm a sucker for well-developed, unlikable characters playing head games with each other and exploiting/competing in positions of power, which is pretty much what happens in this book. </b> Almost to the point it could be Shakespearean. Think Othello being manipulated by Iago when it comes to O's relationship with Desdemona. Except this isn't a romance or really something that's heavily suggestive in that scheme. Not in the least (though you could fill a bucket with the sexual tension in this book). <b>Relationships are manipulated and manipulative. Every single character here has their own aims and desires and they'll do whatever they feel necessary to get what they want. Or protect whom they need to in some cases.</b><br /><br />I'm still shocked that this book pulled me in as much as it did. I had no idea what I was signing up for when I picked up this book and I ended up completely blindsided for the experience.<br /><br />The story on its surface seems like a stereotypical one: you have a cheerleading team that's led by a very strong social butterfly (Beth) for a team captain and her best friend (Addy). Addy is the narrator for this book, and by every measure of description - Addy and Beth's relationship is inseparable. Beth's the queen, Addy's her partner in crime. Their relationship is a formidable one and not necessarily challenged by their peers. At least until a new coach comes into town.<br /><br />The new coach is a young, seemingly powerful woman that gradually wins over the team's affections with her no-nonsense approach to the drills and performances of the team. But it's also her lax demeanor that draws the girls in and allows them to bond with her. Abbott's descriptions for the performances by the team unfolds like watching a dance - I loved the descriptions here, even if sometimes they seem a bit superfluous. <br /><br />My enjoyment's probably heightened there because:<br /><br />1. The descriptions seem so vivid that they're artistically drawn in the prose.<br /><br />2. I'm a graduate in exercise science so the attention given to their bodies and movements really captivated me. I haven't seen that in a lot of texts like this and Abbott portrays it very well.<br /><br />3. The portrayal syncs truthfully with the character voice, for better and worse. Addy's character matches her voice for depictions of events and performances in her witness - she's a spoiled teenage girl with a dark streak and surrounded by other characters who are just as flawed as she is. This is especially well noted in the audio reading of this book. Addy's reflections are indulging for senses and symbolic parallels, yet flawed at the same time. So it worked for me.<br /><br />The coach seems to win everyone over, except Beth. Beth hates the coach with a furious passion, one Addy can't quite put her finger on for certain measures. At first Addy thinks it's because Beth has lost her former power as the team's captain, but it becomes more as Beth's hatred becomes personal, borderline obsessive over the Coach's life and secrets that are kept between the girls.<br /><br />Then the unthinkable happens to someone in their circles, and it throws the coach and the girls into a tailspin. Addy finds herself front and center in the midst of a game of secrets, lies, and manipulations. She's a puppet trying to find out who's pulling her strings and why. I LOVED that aspect of it. The story unfolds like a whodunit mystery but at the same time a dark series of power struggles and sensual tension that builds with a swelling crest up until the "a-ha" moment comes about. I didn't know what route it would go until the last possible point, and it made sense as far as the lying and manipulations were concerned on behalf of multiple parties.<br /><br />Addy's relationship with the coach is palpable, and the Coach, while she seems admirable on the surface, ends up not being much more mature than the students she teaches as her flaws come to the forefront, and she has her own motivations to lie. I saw that as intentional and not a flaw of the text itself. Whether the Coach's motivations are to protect herself or others remains to be seen, and Addy's left to the task of uncovering that on her own. Of course, Addy's pretty much a pawn in some measures, because she's played multiple times between people. It's also revealed that Addy knows Beth is far more twisted for intentions than one would think, to a point that Beth has this hate/love game that she makes Abby play - one piece at a time. <br /><br />While this read might seem cliche on the surface, it's really many layers of a drama built on top of an established stereotype, to be honest. It doesn't mince the fact that these characters are flawed, nor does it push the cast as worthy of emulation - rather stuck in their own delusions. Addy, Beth, and the rest of the crew are all flawed, and standing high on a precipice of denial, indulgence and power, with an event that rattles and threatens to break their foundation. (And I like the parallel that's drawn to the cheerleading routines with this aspect.) It's certainly seen (and felt) with respect to Addy's character, as she tries to navigate the confusion and conflict she feels over events even while she's a character with her own twisted justifications for things. She's also more than willing to hurt whom she has to in order to seek the answers she wants.<br /><br />And OMG, Beth. Can we talk about Beth? She's probably the character that's love to hate here. She's tugging strings and she doesn't care, but the text manages to give her human qualities in the scheme of things, even if you're not sure why she does the things she does up until a certain point, especially when Addy starts revealing things from their past that suggests her own callousness that could run parallel with Beth's manipulations. The two are really more similar than you'd think from the beginning of the work, and neither one of them are necessarily more "moral" than the other. It's just a matter of seeing why they act and react the way they do. Even then, there are points where it leaves you guessing because you're not exactly sure why their loyalties still lie to each other, but yet they're still willing to hurt and manipulate their close relationship. It's a power play, and the fact it's done by these teenage girls who have such social standing is scary enough to think about, especially when it comes to their coach and what they know and how they choose to dance with that information. There's some suspension of disbelief to be had, and I think Abbott works it well - but for some readers, that suspension may not be enough to drive some of the strong symbolic qualities and overarching themes this work has to offer. And that's a shame, because when it hits the ground running, it does remarkably well. I saw it, and I loved it. <br /><br />The ending of the work is hit or miss for events. I thought it was a bit quick for the resolution and even then, there's not a fulfilling sense of vindication for the wrongs that are committed. Then again, I don't think it was meant to be that kind of story, as this cast of fools pretty much end up with a queen and you're watching how all of them collide and function in this bubble, along with the "why".<br /><br />In the end, "Dare Me" answers the question of how far its cast will go to be on top, or risk losing it all, even if it has deadly consequences. I thought the book was brilliant for what it chose to show, with some rough edges and suspensions to take into consideration. I would read this again, readily, for how dark, twisted, and vivid the presentation came across in Abbott's narrative.<br /><br />Overall score: 4.5/5 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="8906289895736605062" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

(The first time I heard about <i>Buffy</i> ever I said "Vampires in high school, are you shitting me? Why would I want to watch that?") But I was powering my way through <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9596460.The_End_of_Everything' title='The End of Everything by Megan Abbott' rel='nofollow noopener'>The End of Everything</a>, slack-jawed, eye-peeled and all agog, and at the back there was a reader's guide (horrible and useless), an author's interview (you're.....glad that Older Lizzie still feels the charm of that family? Uhh. Did you read your own book</span>
  <span id="freeText5787157328127620552" style="display:none">"Noir <i>cheerleaders?"</i> I thought. "Sure, I love Abbott, but no, really, not for me." (The first time I heard about <i>Buffy</i> ever I said "Vampires in high school, are you shitting me? Why would I want to watch that?") But I was powering my way through <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9596460.The_End_of_Everything" title="The End of Everything by Megan Abbott" rel="nofollow noopener">The End of Everything</a>, slack-jawed, eye-peeled and all agog, and at the back there was a reader's guide (horrible and useless), an author's interview (you're.....glad that Older Lizzie still feels the charm of that family? Uhh. Did you read your own book?) and the prologue plus the first chapter of this book. <br /><br /><i>It was something. Don't say it wasn't. ....We're all the same under our skin, aren't we? We're all wanting things we don't understand. Things we can't even name. The yearning so deep, like pinions over our hearts.</i><br /><br />To say I was "hooked" was an understatement. I was landed, gaffed and dressed like the marlin in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2165.The_Old_Man_and_the_Sea" title="The Old Man and the Sea by Ernest Hemingway" rel="nofollow noopener">The Old Man and the Sea</a>. I have SHIT TO DO and now I'm going to spend the entire evening reading about noir cheerleaders. <div id="review_1479455195" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1479455195" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1479455195?book_show_action=true">Dec 25, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=traditional-fiction">traditional-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1479455195" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15203980960761897685">This is my second Megan Abbott, and it won't be my last. There's something about her razor-sharp writing that is so damn compelling and interesting that goes well beyond the subject matter.<br/><br/>I'll be honest. I don't care too much about cheerleaders and cheerleading, but when you mix a near Machiavellian cruelty to the scene, with all the world-weariness of 14 year old girls, and write the living fuck out of it, it becomes mesmerizing. <br/><br/>A lot of people say that Abbott writes Noir, and it is definitel</span>
  <span id="freeText15203980960761897685" style="display:none">This is my second Megan Abbott, and it won't be my last. There's something about her razor-sharp writing that is so damn compelling and interesting that goes well beyond the subject matter.<br /><br />I'll be honest. I don't care too much about cheerleaders and cheerleading, but when you mix a near Machiavellian cruelty to the scene, with all the world-weariness of 14 year old girls, and write the living fuck out of it, it becomes mesmerizing. <br /><br />A lot of people say that Abbott writes Noir, and it is definitely her writing's strength. Sharp lines, stark definitions everywhere, and a deep undercurrent running through the tale. Was it suicide? Was it murder? How is Beth involved? Is the Coach guilty? It's all questions and blinders, and it never sinks into a normal murder mystery. Our narrator Addy so well-crafted that I doubt I'll ever forget her. <br /><br />Everyone's complex. It's hard not to make a serious connection to Lolita, from the cover of the novel to the layers and layers of personality within Addy and all the versions of Beth and Coach French to which we are treated.<br /><br />It's a mystery. But it's also the heaviest novel about cheerleading I've ever read.<br /><br />Indeed, that I ever want to read. The competition is there, of course, but the way all these girls get prepared as if for war, punishing themselves far worse than they punish each other, is just as bad as any of the most intense sports-competition stories I've ever read/watched/or experienced.<br /><br />The microcosm is damn oppressive. It's easy to imagine transporting these girls to an upscale brothel in the 50's under the complex and caring touch of a well-meaning madam, or cutting off a tit to be that most excellent archer of Amazonian fame for the glory of Diana. *sigh* Disturbing. Glorious.<br /><br />The fact is, the writing is so damn good that I fell into the story despite myself. It's truly brilliant in that respect, but I still don't care for the actual subject. My Bad!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15203980960761897685" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_433069982" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/433069982" />
    <a title="N" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4130257-n"><img alt="N" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/433069982?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="N" class="user" itemprop="url" name="N" href="/user/show/4130257-n">N</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer433069982&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer433069982" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17542412210092990670">Ugh. This one left me so frustrated. <br/><br/>The storyline centers around the struggles of a teenage cheerleader who gets sucked into the warped world of her adult coach's sordid love triangle, all while battling the usual teenage issues. The description on the back of the book is much better and sounded really promising, so I snatched this one up.<br/><br/>First things first. I went into this with a critical eye because I was a competition cheerleader for years. When Abbott first describes the girls, she basical</span>
  <span id="freeText17542412210092990670" style="display:none">Ugh. This one left me so frustrated. <br /><br />The storyline centers around the struggles of a teenage cheerleader who gets sucked into the warped world of her adult coach's sordid love triangle, all while battling the usual teenage issues. The description on the back of the book is much better and sounded really promising, so I snatched this one up.<br /><br />First things first. I went into this with a critical eye because I was a competition cheerleader for years. When Abbott first describes the girls, she basically says they do nothing but shake their asses. Yet, magically, after a few weeks with Coach, they are an award-caliber team who can stunt on an expert level. Yeah, that doesn't happen.<br /><br />Second, the language just killed me. I work with teenagers all day, every day. The girls in this book do not sound like any teenagers I've ever encountered, unless they were teleported from some bizarre Clockwork Orange lingo land. Some of the random slang made me wonder if I was on drugs myself. <br /><br />The characters started out decently, at least in concept, but I really had no connection to any of them. Even Addy, who is the freaking narrator, was an enigma wrapped in a mystery. As Beth points out at one point, Addy doesn't even know herself. And, lest you get confused, I don't mean that in a good, "Who am I?" YA thematic way. I mean, literally, Addy has no clue who the hell she is, why she's doing any of the things she gets involved in, why she likes anyone at all in the book (when they're all so unlikeable) or basically why she's even alive at this point. <br /><br />Fourth, I really thought I might have an aneurysm when Coach invited the girls over (all of whom are inexplicably in love with Coach in a psuedo-lesbian manner, even though Coach is clearly emotionally abusive to them) and everyone just breaks out the white wine like it's nothing. I was also sick of Coach's White Whine through out the novel. WE GET IT. You've got an boring marriage. You've got a husband that would work himself to the bone to give you everything you've ever wanted. Talk about first world problems.<br /><br />Finally, because I realize this is getting long, what is left unsaid int his novel is not at all mysterious and thought-provoking. It's just poor storytelling. The Coach-girls relationship makes no sense and was rushed and underdeveloped. The weird obsession Addy has with Matt French seemed like it was going towards some kind of betrayal/affair angle, but went nowhere. The murder went nowhere (and kind of seemed like a rip off of the movie Unfaithful). All of the sudden, Beth, who had been painted as an unconscionable sociopath throughout the novel, is meant to appear sympathetic as she "forces' Addy to confront herself and her inner demons. And at one point the girls had a little lesbian tussle in the woods, which was apparently a BIGFUCKINGDEAL, even though Abbott couldn't be bothered describing it or the girls' feelings about it beyond some vague, surface imagery. <br /><br />Had the ending not been so terrible, I would have rated it a little higher. I did love the concept and I loved the way Abbott doesn't shy away from the cruelty behind girl relationships. However, it seemed like Abbott was either confused or just gave up at one point and phoned it in from there.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17542412210092990670" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_805107454" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/805107454" />
    <a title="Julie Ehlers" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2120450-julie-ehlers"><img alt="Julie Ehlers" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1446217915p2/2120450.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/805107454?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie Ehlers" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie Ehlers" href="/user/show/2120450-julie-ehlers">Julie Ehlers</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780316097789" href="/book/show/16131023-dare-me">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer805107454" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18079821743701961450"><b>The Good:</b> This book was exactly what I was expecting: a noirish thriller, dark and somewhat edgy, full of mean girls, and advancing the notion that the experiences we have in high school are some of the most dramatic and significant of our lives (which for some of us, I guess, they are). Entertaining, propulsive, and well written on its own terms (that last bit sounds like a backhanded compliment, but for this book it isn't—if you read it you'll see what I mean). Will this book change your life?</span>
  <span id="freeText18079821743701961450" style="display:none"><b>The Good:</b> This book was exactly what I was expecting: a noirish thriller, dark and somewhat edgy, full of mean girls, and advancing the notion that the experiences we have in high school are some of the most dramatic and significant of our lives (which for some of us, I guess, they are). Entertaining, propulsive, and well written on its own terms (that last bit sounds like a backhanded compliment, but for this book it isn't—if you read it you'll see what I mean). Will this book change your life? Uh, no. Frankly, if you give this book five stars, you probably need to expand your reading horizons. But if this is the kind of book you're looking for, this is the one you should read.<br /><br /><b>The Bad:</b> Oh, the language! It really worked my nerves sometimes. Make no mistake—it's clear that Megan Abbott is using language quite carefully to achieve a certain effect, and it works, but that doesn't mean I liked it. She makes up her own idioms. One character didn't turn white as a sheet, for instance—she "sheeted white." And the cheerleaders' faces aren't red, they're "tomato-bursting." And all the hyphenation! It was a bit much: "Stone-sinking." "Matchstick-snapped." "Boot-braced." And, of course,"Sparkle-slitted." (Uh....)<br /><br /><b>The Indifferent:</b> There seem to be people on Goodreads complaining that this book is unrealistic and the characters are unlikable. All that is definitely true. If you want likable and realistic, this is not the book for you. But if you just want to have a little no-commitment fun, you can do worse than this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18079821743701961450" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3108620305" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kelly (and the Book Boar)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kelly (and the Book Boar)" href="/user/show/11183856-kelly-and-the-book-boar">Kelly (and the Book Boar)</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3108620305" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14322432888548227571">Find all of my reviews at: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://52bookminimum.blogspot.com/'>http://52bookminimum.blogspot.com/</a><br/><br/><b>
  <i>"Cheer taught me to trust my girls to catch me when I fall. It showed me how to be a leader."</i>
</b><br/><br/>I read <i>Dare Me</i> nearly five years ago and had a mediocre reaction to it. Then the previews for the television series started appearing and I realized that I remembered hardly anything aside from (a) cheerleaders that were (b) Megan Abbott’s signature mean girl type of teen. Old Lady Brain. So debilitating : ( I set the DVR up to record the s</span>
  <span id="freeText14322432888548227571" style="display:none">Find all of my reviews at: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://52bookminimum.blogspot.com/">http://52bookminimum.blogspot.com/</a><br><br><b>
  <i>"Cheer taught me to trust my girls to catch me when I fall. It showed me how to be a leader."</i>
</b><br><br>I read <i>Dare Me</i> nearly five years ago and had a mediocre reaction to it. Then the previews for the television series started appearing and I realized that I remembered hardly anything aside from (a) cheerleaders that were (b) Megan Abbott’s signature mean girl type of teen. Old Lady Brain. So debilitating : ( I set the DVR up to record the show and then by sheer luck was offered a chance for a review copy of the book and decided to give it another go. Well . . . . . .<br><br><a href="https://lunapic.com" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1577715538ra/28688906.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Or, at minimum, a wrongreader because holy crap. WTF was I expecting the first time around? I can’t imagine it going any other direction and it was goooooooood. Like thank God I don’t have daughters like this trainwreck type of good, but oh so good. The TV version was highly disturbing as well. And only one episode in! <br><br>Recommended to those who don’t like their pubescent leads full of sugar and spice and everything nice and their cheerleading . . . . . <br><br><a href="https://lunapic.com" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1577715538ra/28688905.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><i>Copy provided by Little Brown in exchange for an honest review.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="14322432888548227571" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_689580076" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/689580076" />
    <a title="Robert" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5537205-robert"><img alt="Robert" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505620458p2/5537205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/689580076?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert" href="/user/show/5537205-robert">Robert</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/14062212-dare-me">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Robert by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/405390-kemper">Kemper</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5537205-robert?shelf=kindle-deals">kindle-deals</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5537205-robert?shelf=robert-s-reads">robert-s-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer689580076" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6354488525040129141">Let’s be clear from the get go. If you want a more traditional review with a book summary, plot synopsis, and a character family tree, and then possibly a discussion on what the author was trying to accomplish in DARE ME and whether or not she actually achieved her goals, then you’ll probably just want to slide it on back and move on to the next review. Because I’m about as non-traditional as they come. Instead, I like discussing how a book made me feel, or didn’t feel, discussing writing insigh</span>
  <span id="freeText6354488525040129141" style="display:none">Let’s be clear from the get go. If you want a more traditional review with a book summary, plot synopsis, and a character family tree, and then possibly a discussion on what the author was trying to accomplish in DARE ME and whether or not she actually achieved her goals, then you’ll probably just want to slide it on back and move on to the next review. Because I’m about as non-traditional as they come. Instead, I like discussing how a book made me feel, or didn’t feel, discussing writing insights where appropriate, tossing around similes and metaphors like used car parts in a Dumpster, and talking about my overall experience with a book, while taking into account my own knowledge of writing and reading and plain old random shit. In other words, I don’t really know what I’m doing, but I have a damn good time being ignorant.<br /><br />So I’ll start with this: Teenage girls are bitches and badasses. A female praying mantis treats her mate better than high school girls treat each other. And each manages to accomplish this task with a smile on her face and nothing but love in her heart, right before she brings out the icepick and wields it around the same way a construction worker might employ a two-by-four in the middle of a construction zone. <br /><br />One might argue the mystery was a bit thin, but this book transcended the typical books in this genre, and proved there’s more to a mystery than just the identity of the killer and the resolution of the crime. Instead, this was more about cheerleaders and their penchant to attack one another with vengeance, high school drama that unfolded before me on the page in pinks and purples and shades of red, and the extremes captains and coaches go to all in the name of victory. Yes, cheerleading is a sport, and in some parts of the country it’s mentioned in the Sunday prayers along with football and your best friends Jim Bob and Clara Valentine.<br /><br />The shower scene in the girl’s locker room at the beginning of this tale reminded me of all the times growing up that I would have practically handed over bodily organs to be given even a brief glimpse behind that steel curtain. But what made this story really click for me was the relationship between Addy Hanlon and Beth Cassidy, gal pals that dance a relationship tango better left choreographed to the professionals. And proves there’s much more to a relationship than what’s shown to the public.<br /><br />This tale was about as easy to swallow as cotton candy stuffed with razor blades, and now that I know what’s behind the pom-poms I wish I could give it all back, since more knowledge isn’t always the key to happiness, as this story aptly proves.<br /><br /><a href="http://www.robertdowns.net/blog/" rel="nofollow noopener">Cross-posted at Robert's Reads</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="6354488525040129141" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1400174838" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1400174838" />
    <a title="destini mia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13742215-destini-mia"><img alt="destini mia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499903277p2/13742215.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1400174838?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="destini mia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="destini mia" href="/user/show/13742215-destini-mia">destini mia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1400174838" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2385145338418554882"><b>This is girls at their ugliest.</b><br/><i>
  <blockquote>That's what people never understand: They see us as hard little pretty things, brightly lacquered and sequin-studded, and they laugh, they mock, they arouse themselves. They miss everything.<br/>You see, these glitters are war paint, it's feathers and claws, it's blood sacrifice. </blockquote>
</i><br/>If I could only use one quote to sum up the entirety of this book, it would be: <i>"there's something dangerous about the boredom of teenage girls</i>. When the glitter's gone and the makeup's was</span>
  <span id="freeText2385145338418554882" style="display:none"><b>This is girls at their ugliest.</b><br /><i>
  <blockquote>That's what people never understand: They see us as hard little pretty things, brightly lacquered and sequin-studded, and they laugh, they mock, they arouse themselves. They miss everything.<br />You see, these glitters are war paint, it's feathers and claws, it's blood sacrifice. </blockquote>
</i><br />If I could only use one quote to sum up the entirety of this book, it would be: <i>"there's something dangerous about the boredom of teenage girls</i>. When the glitter's gone and the makeup's washed off, when you can finally see beneath the girl, <b>you realize how cruel a creature girls can be</b>. It's dark and unrelenting. It shows girls as something other than victims. <b>As something terrible and unforgiving</b>. <br /><br />Cheerleading is everything to these girls. The power, the prestige, the ability to <i>"make my body do anything"</i>. Beth is Top Girl and Addy is her faithful sidekick. They rule the school along with their cheer peers. Sounds like a typically all-American type story. <br />Until a new coach comes to town, stealing everyone's hearts and loyalties, except for Beth's. Unhappy at the new arrival, Beth is instantly suspicious at Coach's motives and will do just about anything to be Top Girl again. <br /><i>
  <blockquote>And Coach gave it all to us. We never had it before her. So can you blame me for wanting to keep it? To fight for it, to the end?</blockquote>
</i><br />I can't help but think about how apt "girls" is in the description of these, well, <i>girls</i>. The label goes past their age, past the experience (or lack of) the word girls connotes. I feel like it encompasses their fragility, innocence, power, cunning, and <b>ability to make someone do anything for them</b>. On the outside, they're <i>just</i> girls. But in the inside, they're <b>girls</b>. Capable of <u>anything</u>. <br /><i>
  <blockquote>We're all the same under our skin, aren't we?<br />We're all wanting things we don't understand. Things we can't even name. The yearning so deep, like pinions over our hearts.</blockquote>
</i><br /><br /><i>Dare Me</i> fits in with the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/21480930.Gone_Girl" title="Gone Girl by Gillian Flynn" rel="nofollow noopener">Gone Girl</a>'s and the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/66559.Sharp_Objects" title="Sharp Objects by Gillian Flynn" rel="nofollow noopener">Sharp Objects</a>' by showing girls/women in a different light. Not as victims, but as <b>powerful. Cunning. Deceptive. In control. </b> Not to say that the things they do aren't wrong, but it's a refreshing change from the usually <i>men = bad guys . . . women = good girls</i>. Not always. Sometimes they manage to outsmart, out-decieve, out-abuse the best. They can dupe the smartest, strongest, oldest and wisest. <br /><i>
  <blockquote>I guess I'd been waiting forever, my palm raised. <br />Waiting for someone to take my girl body and turn it out, steel from the inside, make things matter for me, like never before.</blockquote>
</i><br />Everything about this book is complex. The relationships, the storyline, everything. It's so <b>toxic</b>. <u>It's girls at their worst</u>. <br /><br />If there was ever an unreliable narrator, it's Abby. Beth said it perfectly, she counts on other people to say the truth about her. She ignore what she's doing, the pain she's causing, the lies she's spreading. In comparison to Beth, she almost seems innocent. The timid sidekick. Beth's shadow. Not quite Top Girl. But she's as bad as the rest of them. You as the reader have to discern this for yourself, because Abby pushes this part of herself aside and you end up getting lost in what she's feeling. <br /><i>
  <blockquote>"You have always been soft to these things, Addy," she says.</blockquote>
</i><br />Beth was even more of enigma. What I wouldn't give to be able to pick her brain. She was always wanting, always reaching, doing whatever she could to get what she desired. Only, we don't know what she wants or at what lengths she willing to go to achieve it.<br /><i>
  <blockquote>First, my eyes falling to the floor, I see a glint of hair twinning in the weave of the rug.<br />Then stepping forward, I see more.<br />"Addy," she whispers. "Addy . . . is it like I thought?"</blockquote>
</i><br />It almost seems <b>obvious</b> what happened. Like the author and characters were goading me. Purposefully emphasizing one idea over the other so if I chose the obvious one, it would be because <u>it's obvious</u>. But if I chose the one that the author wasn't leading me towards, it would be because <u>hello, the other one was just too obvious</u>.<br /><br />Well, in the end, all my guesses were wrong. Which makes for the best kind of reads, in my opinion. <br /><i>
  <blockquote> "We are never deceived," she says, her voice deep and ringing. "We deceive ourselves."</blockquote>
</i><br /><br />I really liked this book, it was dark and gritty and kind of breaks the mold but it wasn't without faults. I love when authors can make the most outlandish storylines seem realistic. I didn't get that with <i>Dare Me</i>. Yeah, I was interested in what was happening, but I didn't believe it. It didn't have that degree of realism that really makes a story stand apart. <br /><br />I also felt like the story was so focused on the girls, the coach, the drama that it almost felt like nothing else existed. Parents? Mentioned no more than twice. I mean, what happened to the rest of the world? I felt like this was some kind of twilight zone world, completely cut off from everyone else and reality. <br /><br />All in all, a terrifying story on the boredom of teenage girls. A solid, solid read.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2385145338418554882" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1006443867" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1006443867" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2314568-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1463021489p2/2314568.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1006443867?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/2314568-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780316203234" href="/book/show/13521387-dare-me">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2314568-steve?shelf=noir">noir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2314568-steve?shelf=read-in-2015">read-in-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2314568-steve?shelf=contemporary-fiction">contemporary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2314568-steve?shelf=suspense-thriller">suspense-thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2314568-steve?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2314568-steve?shelf=ya">ya</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1006443867" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6977983920920404783">3.5 brutal stars.<br/><br/>According to sports injury research, cheerleading is more dangerous than any other sport, with the rate of such injuries doubling between 1990 and 2002. For members of the Cheer Squad, captained by Beth Cassidy, there is less chance of being hurt by doing cheer stunts than by getting between Beth and Colette French, the new cheer coach. Beth rules the roost and she will do whatever it takes to anchor her position at the top of this pyramid.<br/><br/>The story is told from the viewpoint of</span>
  <span id="freeText6977983920920404783" style="display:none">3.5 brutal stars.<br /><br />According to sports injury research, cheerleading is more dangerous than any other sport, with the rate of such injuries doubling between 1990 and 2002. For members of the Cheer Squad, captained by Beth Cassidy, there is less chance of being hurt by doing cheer stunts than by getting between Beth and Colette French, the new cheer coach. Beth rules the roost and she will do whatever it takes to anchor her position at the top of this pyramid.<br /><br />The story is told from the viewpoint of Addy Hanlon, Beth’s life-long lieutenant. Cheerleading is of such importance to her that high school only exists so these girls can have a cheerleading squad. Coach French’s appearance offers the possibility of the Squad to move from a bunch of girls who shaking their pom-poms into a competitive team of athletes. Beth sees the threat to her power immediately. Coach French initially behaves as any professional sport coach would be expected to behave – driven, disciplined, hard-shelled - until many of the members begin to see the progress they are making. At that point, the coach becomes part coach, part parent, part defiler of youth. All the women who make up this squad are “mean,” no one is exempt from the venom so easily and frequently sprayed and everyone is injured by the atmosphere that is allowed to fester. When a tragic event occurs, Beth is offered the opportunity to regain her place as “owner” of the Cheerleaders. Addy is caught between loyalty to her friend, her coach, and her team. She is not innocent in any of the events which seem to envelope her, but neither is she party to the subterfuge that sparked those events.<br /><br />The book shows the writing talent of Ms. Abbott. She highlights well the tension, confusion, anger, angst, and isolation experienced by all the girls. The world she creates is largely parent-free, which is difficult to comprehend. Addy and Beth are out at all hours of the night, leave home whenever they want, yet are never confronted by anyone in authority for their behavior. They ingest various substances, most illegal, rarely eat (or even retain what they do eat) but have no ill-effects from such behavior. For athletes in training for the “Big Game” where “the scout for regionals” will be in attendance, they show little commitment to staying healthy for that event. This undercurrent caused the story to be less believable and less plausible.<br /><br />The ending is well done, having drawn the reader in an entirely different direction until the moment when the curtain is lifted high enough to reveal the hidden truth about each of the characters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6977983920920404783" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('SpGHf8n0nDXQ532kmm/JZQQecBKljtxB8WdLamNVyQd8TSfkgLmb65E99pfB5MSonjJN9Wv94UO0le2In61tJA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('xG7mg0YbYNjkIP57y+3QNO8rdlXLlJ8+8QG449WPWsbyskYYD1ZnBqX6dUiQZt35dQdLsgXnojy08x4BKXf+5Q==')})
  }

          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12501215211689251567">Megan Abbott is the Edgar®-winning author of the novels Die a Little, Queenpin, The Song Is You, Bury Me Deep, The End of Everything, Dare Me, The Fever, You Will Know Me and Give Me Your Hand.<br/><br/>Abbott is co-showrunner, writer and executive producer of DARE ME, the TV show adapated from her novel. She was also a staff writer on HBO's THE DEUCE. Her writing has also appeared in the New York Times, th</span>
  <span id="freeText12501215211689251567" style="display:none">Megan Abbott is the Edgar®-winning author of the novels Die a Little, Queenpin, The Song Is You, Bury Me Deep, The End of Everything, Dare Me, The Fever, You Will Know Me and Give Me Your Hand.<br /><br />Abbott is co-showrunner, writer and executive producer of DARE ME, the TV show adapated from her novel. She was also a staff writer on HBO's THE DEUCE. Her writing has also appeared in the New York Times, the Guardian, Wall Street Journal, the Los Angeles Times Magazine, The Believer and the Los Angeles Review of Books.<br /><br />Born in the Detroit area, she graduated from the University of Michigan and received her Ph.D. in English and American literature from New York University. She has taught at NYU, SUNY and the New School University and has served as the John Grisham Writer in Residence at The University of Mississippi.<br /><br />She is also the author of a nonfiction book, The Street Was Mine: White Masculinity in Hardboiled Fiction and Film Noir, and the editor of A Hell of a Woman, an anthology of female crime fiction. She is currently developing two of her novels, Dare Me and The Fever, for television.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12501215211689251567" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
